CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Fehr & Peers conducted a study to assess the potential traffic impacts associated with the proposed
"Major Changes in Allowable Land Use" being submitted to the voters of the City of Redondo Beach
pursuant to Article XXVII of the City Charter (Measure DD). The proposed "Major Changes in Allowable
Land Use" consist of amendments to the City's certified Coastal Land Use Plan ("CLUP") Zoning
Ordinance for the Coastal Zone (Coastal Zoning Ordinance or "CZO") approved by the Redondo Beach
City Council in August, 2005, May, 2008 and April, 2010. These CLUP and CZO amendments are
collectively referred to as the "Project" where appropriate. The proposed CLUP and CZO amendments
affect the Harbor and Pier areas of the City, the AES Power Plant site, and properties along the west side
of North Catalina Avenue (the Catalina Avenue or Catalina Corridor properties), Geographically, the area
is bounded by the Pacific Ocean, Herondo Street, North Catalina Avenue, and Torrance Boulevard.
Figure 1 shows the area directly affected by the Project in relation to the area street network. The
following analysis is intended to meet the requirements of Section 27.4(b) of Article XXVII of the City
Charter of Redondo Beach. Section 27.4(b) requires that ballot materials mailed to registered voters prior
to the election include a comparison of the traffic impacts of the Project with the "As Built Condition" (i.e.
pre-Project conditions) as defined in Section 27.2(b) of Article XXVII and with traffic impacts associated
with build-out of the existing applicable land use designations and zoning classifications.

1.1 PROJECT DESCRIPTION
The Project would substantially revise and augment the policies and land use designations in the City's
existing CLUP and CZO for the Harbor/Pier, AES Power Plant and Catalina Corridor areas, as more fully
described in the project description prepared by City staff. With respect to traffic impacts, the principal
effects of the proposed CLUP and CZO Amendments, as compared to the existing City CLUP and
zoning, would be to impose extensive additional restrictions and standards for future development (or
redevelopment) within the Project area, including a development cap of 400,000 square feet (sf) of
additional development in the Harbor/Pier area. The land uses permitted by the CLUP and CZO
Amendments fall into a number of categories, but are predominately commercial in nature. For purposes
of determining traffic impacts, the uses permitted under the CLUP and CZO amendments would fall into
several general categories. Table 1 details the amount of net-new development in each traffic generation
category reasonably expected to occur under the CLUP and CZO amendments.

Vehicular access to the affected properties would remain as it is under the "As-Built" circulation system,
with access to the Harbor/Pier and AES Power Plant parcels continuing to be mainly off of Harbor
Boulevard, and access to the Catalina Corridor Parcels to continuing to be mainly off of Catalina and
Francisca Avenues.

1.2 REGULATORY FRAMEWORK
This study conforms to analysis methodologies and impact criteria from the following two regulatory
elements:

    ·   City Charter, Article XXVII­ Redondo Beach voters approved Measure DD, which added Article
        XXVII, entitled "Major Changes in Allowable Land Use" to the City Charter, in November 2008.
        Article XXVII requires voter approval for certain "Major Changes in Allowable Land Use" to the
        City's General Plan, Coastal Land Use Plan, and/or the City's zoning ordinance or coastal zoning
        ordinance. Section 27.4(b) of Article XXVII provides that when an election for any "Major Change
        in Allowable Land Use" is held, the sample ballot materials provided to voters must contain
        certain information on anticipated traffic impacts resulting from the proposed change. Section
        27.4(b), does not identify the specific methodologies and impact criteria that should be included in

        the traffic study. However, for purposes of this report, guidance as to appropriate methodologies
        has been drawn from Section 27.5 of Article XXVII, which specifies information to be supplied by
        the applicant in any application for a Major Change in Allowable Land Use that is subject to
        Article XXVII. Additional impact criteria were identified for intersections and roadway segments
        (Corridors) defined as "critical" in Sections 27.2(d) and 27.5(a)(4) of Article XXVII.

    ·   Redondo Beach Circulation Element ­ In 2009, the City conducted a major review of existing and
        projected future traffic conditions in connection with an update of the Circulation Element of the
        City's general plan. The draft Circulation Element identified impact criteria were used, along with
        criteria drawn from Article XXVII, to asses potential Project impacts in this study. The 2009
        Circulation Element update is awaiting formal City Council approval.

1.3 STUDY SCOPE

1.3.1   Study Area

As illustrated in Figure 1, the study area is defined by the area contained within 3,000 feet of the parcel
boundary of the Project Site. Based on the requirements of Article XXVII, eight study intersections
(illustrated in Figure 2) located within the study area were selected for analysis:

    1) Pacific Coast Highway (PCH) & Herondo Street/Anita Street ("Critical" designation in Article
       XXVII)
    2) PCH & Catalina Avenue ("Critical" designation in Article XXVII)
    3) Catalina Avenue & Francisca Avenue (Operates at LOS E or worse during one or both peak
       hours under "As-Built Conditions", therefore included for analysis based on requirements of
       Article XXVII)

In adherence to Article XXVII, and illustrated in Figure 1, the study also examined conditions on portions
of roadway designated as "Critical Corridors" in Article XXVII. The portions of the "Critical Corridors"
located within 3,000 feet of the parcel boundary of the Project Site are listed below:

    1) PCH between Aviation Boulevard and Torrance Boulevard
    2) PCH between Torrance Boulevard and Topaz Street
    3) Catalina Avenue between PCH and Beryl Street
    4) Catalina Avenue between Beryl Street and Torrance Boulevard
    5) Herondo Street between Harbor Drive and PCH
    6) Anita Street between PCH and Harkness Lane
    7) Torrance Boulevard between its western terminus and PCH
    8) Torrance Boulevard between PCH and Irena Avenue
    9) Prospect Avenue between Anita Street and Diamond Street

1.3.2   Scenarios Analyzed

Traffic impacts were evaluated on a typical weekday during the morning (7:00 to 9:00 AM) peak period
and afternoon (4:00 to 6:00 PM) peak period. The following traffic scenarios have been analyzed as part
of this study:

    ·   "As-Built" Conditions­ Article XXVII requires a comparison of traffic impacts to the "As-Built
        Condition," which is defined as the baseline traffic conditions existing at the time the city either
        issues a notice of preparation of an environmental impact report or otherwise commences
        environmental analysis for a project which includes a Major Change in Allowable Land Use. In
        the case of the proposed CLUP and CZO amendments, this date is assumed to be August, 2007
        (Initial Environmental Study No. 2007-03-IIES-003), when environmental analysis for the major
        CLUP and CZO amendments for the Harbor/Pier area was commenced. Although the CLUP and
        CZO amendments for the AES Power Plant site and Catalina Avenue corridor were adopted in
        2005 (3 ½ years prior to the effective date of Article XXVII), no traffic data was generated for
        these amendments nor is any available for the 2004-2005 time period from other sources. Given
        the absence of any substantial development in these Project sub-areas, however, data utilized in
        determining 2007 "As-Built" Conditions and traffic generation should reasonably reflect 2005
        conditions. The analysis of the "As-Built" traffic conditions is intended to provide a basis for the
        remainder of the study. The "As-Built Conditions" analysis includes an assessment of streets,
        traffic volumes, and operating conditions.

    ·   "As-Built" plus Project Conditions ­ The "As-Built" plus Project Conditions analysis measures "As-
        Built Conditions" with the addition of traffic expected to be generated by development allowed by
        the Project. The incremental impacts of the Project on "As-Built" traffic operating conditions were
        then identified.




                                                                                                          5
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



    ·   Cumulative Base Conditions ­ Future traffic conditions were projected for the year 2030 without
        the Project. The objective of this analysis is to forecast the future traffic growth and intersection
        operating conditions expected to result from general regional growth and projects developed near
        the Project Site by the year 2030. This scenario is used as the baseline against which the
        project's contribution to cumulative traffic impacts is determined.

    ·   Cumulative Base plus Project ­ The cumulative plus project analysis measures future traffic
        conditions with traffic expected to be generated by the project added to the 2030 cumulative base
        traffic conditions. The incremental impacts of the project on future traffic operating conditions
        were then identified.

    ·   Comparison of Project against Existing Applicable Land Use Designations and Zoning
        Classifications. Section 27.4(b) of Article XXVII required a comparison of the "project and its
        traffic impacts" to "existing applicable land use designations and zoning classifications." The
        purpose of this analysis is to compare traffic conditions likely to occur with the Project with those
        likely to occur if the Project is not approved by the voters. This comparative analysis conducted
        in this study may be broken down into two parts.

        a. Comparison of CLUP and CZO amendments to Existing CLUP and Zoning, This analysis
           compares the traffic impacts allowed by the existing CLUP and existing zoning to the impacts
           that would occur with approval of the CLUP and CZO amendments, ignoring all other
           regulatory restrictions likely to affect traffic. This analysis allows a side-by-side comparison of
           the traffic impacts of the existing and proposed CLUP and zoning regulations only. It
           represents a form of "worst-case" analysis in that it shows what would occur if all other
           regulatory constraints on traffic outside of the CLUP and zoning regulations, e.g. standards
           contained in the City's general plan or a specific plan, were removed. Due to the lack of any
           quantifiable limits on development under the existing City CLUP and zoning, a full analysis of
           comparative impacts was not possible. An alternative analysis designed to confirm the
           probable impacts of development under the existing CLUP and CZO was performed.

        b. Comparison of CLUP and CZO amendments with All Existing Regulations. This analysis
           compares the traffic impacts of the Project with impacts reasonably expected to occur under
           all applicable existing regulations affecting building density and intensity, including relevant
           provisions of the land use element of the City's general plan and the Harbor Civic Center
           Specific Plan. This analysis allows a practical comparison of what conditions would likely
           result from approval or rejection of the proposed CLUP or CZO amendments, assuming no
           other major changes in the allowable land use for the Project area.

        These comparison analyses may be found in appendices to this report.

1.4 ORGANIZATION OF REPORT
This report is divided into four chapters, including this introduction. Chapter 2 describes "As Built
conditions" in the study area, including a street inventory, a summary of traffic volumes, and an
assessment of the operating conditions at the study intersections. Chapter 3 describes the methodolo-
gies used to develop traffic forecasts for the cumulative base and cumulative plus project scenarios and
the forecasts themselves, and an assessment of the proposed Project's traffic impacts. Chapter 4
provides a summary of the results. Appendices to this report include the analysis comparing the traffic
impacts of the proposed Project with traffic impacts under existing regulations, as well as details of the
technical analysis.

Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010




CHAPTER 2. AS-BUILT CONDITIONS
This chapter details the comprehensive data collection effort undertaken to provide an assessment of As-
Built conditions in the study area. The assessment of conditions relevant to this study includes a
description of the study area, an inventory of the local street system near the project site, a review of
traffic volumes on these facilities, and an assessment of the existing operating conditions, as they existed
at the time environmental review of the proposed CLUP amendments was commenced, i.e. in August,
2007 (Initial Environmental Study No. 2007-03-IIES-003). A detailed description of these elements is
presented in this chapter.

2.1 AS-BUILT CONDITIONS STREET SYSTEM
The following details the key roadway facilities that serve the project site:

    ·   Anita Street ­ Anita Street is an east/west major arterial that runs east of Pacific Coast Highway
        (PCH) with two lanes in each direction. Between Maria and Prospect Avenue, there is a center
        turning lane. East of Prospect, there are left-turn pockets at most intersections, with a raised
        median. On-street parking is generally permitted on both sides of Anita Street.

    ·   Beryl Street ­ Beryl Street is a southeast-northwest secondary arterial that runs from Harbor
                     th
        Drive to 190 Street. Between Prospect Street and Catalina Avenue, Beryl Street is one lane in
        each direction with a center turning lane. Beryl Street narrows to two lanes east of Flagler Lane.
        On-street parking is permitted between Catalina Avenue and Flagler Lane.

    ·   Catalina Avenue ­ Catalina Avenue is a four-lane north/south secondary arterial that runs from
        PCH near the northern City boundary to Palos Verdes Boulevard at the southern City boundary.
        On-street parking is metered on the west side from Carnelian Street to Torrance Boulevard and
        on the east side from Emerald Street to Pearl Street. A raised median exists between Beryl
        Street and Torrance Boulevard.

    ·   Esplanade ­ Esplanade is a two-lane north/south collector that runs from Catalina Avenue to
        Vista Del Mar. On-street parking is permitted, with meter control on the west side between
        Avenue A and Avenue I as well as the entire east side of Esplanade. From Knob Hill Avenue
        south, Esplanade runs two lanes with a center turn lane and bike lanes on both sides of the
        street.

    ·   Harbor Drive ­ Harbor Drive is a two-lane north/south collector between Herondo Street and
        Beryl Street. North of Herondo Street, this facility continues north as Hermosa Avenue. Metered
        on-street parking and bike lanes are provided.

    ·   Herondo Street ­ Herondo Street is an east/west secondary arterial that runs from PCH to Harbor
        Drive with two lanes in each direction. There is a raised median, and left-turn pockets at most
        intersections. On-street parking is generally permitted on both sides of Herondo Street.

    ·   Pacific Coast Highway (State Route 1) ­ PCH is a four-lane north/south major arterial. Left-turn
        lanes are provided at major intersections and travel speeds are relatively low, as characteristic of
        commercial corridors. A raised median is located south of Avenue H. On-street parking is
        prohibited along short sections of PCH at Torrance Boulevard, Catalina Avenue and Diamond
        Street, and generally permitted elsewhere.

    ·   Torrance Boulevard ­ Torrance Boulevard is a four-lane east/west major arterial that ends in a
        cul-de-sac west of Catalina Avenue. On-street parking is permitted along most of its length in the
        study area.



                                                                                                          7
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



2.2 AS-BUILT CONDITIONS PUBLIC TRANSIT SERVICE
The study area is served by several bus routes operated by four transit operators, including the Los
Angeles County Metropolitan Transportation Authority (Metro), Los Angeles Department of Transportation
Commuter Express (CE), Beach Cities Transit (BCT), and Torrance Transit (TT). The following details
each individual line that serves the study area:

    ·   Metro Line 130 ­ Line 130 provides local service between the Los Cerritos Center in Cerritos and
        Redondo Beach. In the study area, Line 130 travels north and south along Harbor Boulevard and
        Catalina Avenue. Service is provided seven days per week, with weekday peak period headways
        of approximately 20 to 30 minutes.

    ·   Metro Line 232 ­ Line 232 provides local service between the LAX bus center and Downtown
        Long Beach. In the study area, Line 210 travels north and south along PCH. Service is provided
        seven days per week with weekday peak period headways of approximately 10 to 20 minutes.

    ·   CE Line 438 ­ Line 438 provides express service between Downtown Los Angeles and the City
        of Redondo Beach. In the study area, Line 438 travels north and south along Harbor Drive and
        Catalina Avenue. Service is provided Monday through Friday, with peak period headways of
        approximately 15 to 30 minutes.

    ·   BCT Line 102 ­ Line 102 provides local service between the Metro Green Line, the South Bay
        Galleria, and the Redondo Beach Pier. In the study area, Line 102 travels north and south along
        Catalina Avenue and northeast and southwest along Diamond Street. Service is provided seven
        days per week, with weekday peak period headways of approximately 30 to 40 minutes.

    ·   BCT Line 104 ­ Line 104 provides local service between the Del Amo Fashion Center and the
        Redondo Beach Pier. In the study area, Line 104 travels north and south along Catalina Avenue.
        Service is provided Monday through Saturday, with weekday peak period headways of
        approximately 70 minutes.

    ·   BCT Line 109 ­ Line 109 provides local service between the LAX Bus Center, Redondo Beach
        Pier, and Riviera Village. In the study area, Line 109 travels north and south along Catalina
        Avenue. Service is provided seven days per week, with weekday peak period headways of
        approximately 30 to 45 minutes.

    ·   TT Line 3 ­ Line 3 provides local service between Downtown Long Beach and the Redondo
        Beach Pier. In the study area, Line 3 travels east and west along Torrance Boulevard. Service is
        provided seven days per week, with weekday peak period headways of approximately 15
        minutes.

    ·   TT Line 7 ­ Line 7 provides local service between Wilmington and the Redondo Beach Pier. In
        the study area, Line 7 travels east and west along Torrance Boulevard. Service is provided
        Monday through Saturday, with weekday peak period headways of approximately 30 minutes.

2.3 AS-BUILT TRAFFIC CONDITIONS
This section presents the As-Built traffic conditions in terms of peak hour turning movement traffic
volumes for the intersections analyzed in the study, describes the methodology used to assess the traffic
conditions at each intersection, and analyzes the resulting operating conditions at each, indicating
volume-to-capacity (V/C) ratios and levels of service (LOS).




                                                                                                       8
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



2.3.1   Intersection Traffic Volumes

To analyze As-Built Conditions, weekday morning and afternoon peak hour intersection turning
movement counts were conducted at the study intersections in the spring of 2007, while local public
schools were in session. While the As-Built Conditions analysis should reflect an evaluation of conditions
as of August, 2007 (release date of Initial Environmental Study No. 2007-03-IIES-003), because public
schools were not in session at that time, intersection turning movement counts were conducted in the
spring to ensure a conservative "worst case" baseline for the "As-Built" Conditions analysis. Figure 3
illustrates the existing morning and afternoon weekday peak hour traffic volumes at the study
intersections. Traffic count data sheets are provided in Appendix B.

2.3.2   Applicability of As-Built Conditions (2007) to Present Existing Conditions (2010)

While Article XXVII designates that an As-Built Conditions analysis be conducted, an "Existing"
Conditions analysis is a typical baseline for evaluating most projects. In order to ensure the relevance of
the As-Built Conditions analysis (using intersection counts taken in 2007) to existing conditions in 2010, a
comparison was made between 2007 daily roadway segment counts and new daily segment counts
collected in June 2010. Table 2 presents this comparison.


                                                   TABLE 2
                                        2007/2010 COUNT COMPARISON

                      Count Segment                        2007             2010          % Change
        Anita Street
        between Pacific Coast Highway and Rindge          22,100            20,500          -7.2%
        Lane
        Torrance Boulevard
                                                          22,200            23,500          5.9%
        east of Pacific Coast Highway
        Catalina Avenue
                                                          20,100            22,600          12.4%
        between Beryl Street and Torrance Boulevard
        Total                                             64,400            66,600          3.4%



As seen in the table, traffic levels on the three segments varied between 2007 and 2010, with Anita Street
showing a decline in traffic, and Torrance Boulevard and Catalina Avenue showing an increase. In total,
the summed traffic across all three segments increased by 3.4%. Traffic on roadways generally
fluctuates day-to-day, and variations upwards of 15% are not uncommon. The variation between the
2007 and 2010 counts are well within this level of variability. It can therefore be assumed that the As-
Built Condition analysis required by Article XXVII is also reasonably reflective of actual existing conditions
at the present time, i.e. in 2010. (See also Section 2.3.4 below.)

A comparison was also made of segment counts on PCH, a Caltrans facility. 2007 segment counts were
compared to 2008 counts (most recent counts made available by Caltrans.) Table 3 details this count
comparison, showing that average daily traffic declined on PCH between 2007 and 2008.

2.3.3   Intersection Level of Service Methodologies

LOS is a qualitative measure used to describe the condition of traffic flow on the street system, ranging
from excellent conditions at LOS A to overloaded conditions at LOS F. Seven of the eight study
intersections are signalized, and one is two-way stop-controlled. Two types of LOS analysis were
conducted: Intersection Capacity Utilization (ICU), and Highway Capacity Manual (HCM). LOS definitions
for both methodologies are provided in Tables 4 and 5.

Intersection Capacity Utilization

The ICU method of intersection analysis was used to determine the intersection V/C ratio and
corresponding LOS for the turning movements and intersection characteristics at the signalized
intersections. The ICU value is determined by summing the V/C ratio sum of the critical movements, plus
a factor for yellow signal time. Consistent with the analysis in the Circulation Element, the Traffix software
package was used to produce ICU results.

Highway Capacity Manual

The signalized intersections were analyzed using the Operations Methodology for signalized intersections
from the Highway Capacity Manual (HCM) (Transportation Research Board, 2000), which was the current
Highway Capacity Manual as of August, 2007. Intersection delay was calculated, and used to find the
corresponding LOS in Table 5 based on the amount of control delay. The un-signalized intersection was
analyzed using the Two-Way Stop method from the HCM. Delay was calculated based on the worst-case
approach, and used to find the corresponding LOS listed in Table 5. The Synchro software package was
used to produce the HCM results.

Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach

2.3.6   Qualitative Observations of As-Built Conditions

Although Section 27.4(b) of Article XXVII does not specifically require analysis of traffic safety issues or
other qualitative traffic-related issues in the ballot materials provided to voters, Section 27.5(a)(4) requires
that additional analysis of traffic-related issues be provided by the applicant at the time of an application
for a Major Change in Allowable Land Use. In this case, however, Article XXVII had not been enacted at
the time the May, 2008 and August, 2005 CLUP and CZO amendments were proposed. In addition,


                                                                                                             15
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



some of the qualitative issues identified in Section 27.5(a)(4) appear to be directed at analysis of actual
development projects proposed in connection with a Major Change in Allowable Land Use, and not for
changes in area-wide land use regulations. Nevertheless, in order to provide additional information to the
voters in connection with the proposed CLUP amendments, qualitative observations of existing conditions
were made during AM and PM peak periods on an average weekday in June 2010. Observations were
made by circulating through the roadway segments in the study area multiple times during both peak
periods. Given the lack of major changes in the circulation network since 2007 and comparison of 2007
and more recent traffic counts, it is reasonable to assume that these observations are also reasonably
reflect the "As Built Conditions" existing at the time environmental review of the CLUP Amendments
commenced in 2007. The following details the key points for each category of observations suggested
by Section 27.5(a)(4) of Article XXVII:

    ·   Heavy Vehicle Traffic ­ A small amount of heavy vehicle traffic was observed on PCH and on
        Torrance Boulevard. No heavy vehicle traffic was observed on other roadway corridors. No
        issue with heavy vehicle traffic was noted.

    ·   Bus Stops ­ Bus stops were observed every few blocks on PCH. In several locations, bus stops
        do not have a curb cut, so when a bus stops to pick-up or drop-off passengers, through traffic in
        some cases, or a right-turn lane in other cases, is temporarily blocked. For example, during the
        AM peak period, a bus stopped to pick up passengers at the northbound bus stop on Pacific
        Coast Highway just north of Torrance Boulevard. This temporarily blocked one lane of
        northbound through traffic, and contributed to the queuing observed along PCH.

    ·   Intersection and Corridor Oversaturation (Downstream Traffic Queuing Impacts) ­ Heavy traffic
        flows were observed along several points of PCH during AM and PM peak periods. During AM
        peak periods, northbound PCH was heaviest, while during PM peak periods, southbound PCH
        was heaviest, though the flows in both directions were more balanced than during the AM peak
        period. Moderately heavy north and southbound traffic was observed on Catalina Avenue at
        Torrance Boulevard during the PM peak period. Downstream queuing and oversaturation were
        observed at several points along PCH. For example, during the AM and PM peak periods,
        queues about two blocks long were observed along PCH south of Torrance Boulevard
        (northbound traffic) and north of Torrance (southbound traffic).

    ·   Side Street and Driveway Entrances and Exits ­ No major issues related to the location of side
        streets and driveway entrances and exits were observed, as most side streets could be accessed
        via left-turn only lanes off of PCH, and so would not block traffic. The observed exception was at
        Garnett Street, where northbound or southbound vehicles waiting to make a turn onto Garnett
        would block traffic. However, no vehicles were observed making these turns. One vehicle was
        observed making a turn into a driveway from southbound Pacific Coast Highway. This move
        temporarily blocked one southbound through lane, but southbound traffic on PCH was relatively
        light at that point, so did not contribute to queuing.

    ·   Ingress Stacking and Overflowing ­ No issues related to ingress stacking and overflowing were
        observed.

    ·   Left-Turn Lane Queuing and Overflow ­ Overflow from left-turn pockets was observed at a limited
        number of intersections during the PM peak period. The westbound double left pockets at the
        intersection of PCH & Anita Street were observed to regularly fill to their storage capacity, with
        overflow of one or two vehicles into the westbound through lane. The southbound left-turn pocket
        at the intersection of PCH & Torrance Boulevard was also observed overflowing into one of the
        southbound through lanes.

    ·   Traffic, Pedestrian & Bicycle Safety ­ No unsafe conditions were observed. All traffic controls
        were observed to operate according to relevant safety standards. All pedestrian crossings and



                                                                                                        16
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



        on-street bike lanes appeared to be designed to relevant safety standards. Little pedestrian and
        bicycle traffic was observed in the study area.

    While queuing and congestion was observed, the field observations indicated that all roadway
    facilities have been designed and maintained in accordance with the national standards outlined in
    Geometric Design of Highways and Streets, American Association of State Highway and
    Transportation Officials (AASHTO). Traffic signals and signage were observed to be designed in
    accordance with the national standards outlined in the Manual on Uniform Traffic Control Devices
    (MUTCD), Federal Highway Administration (FHA), and the California state standards outlined in the
    Traffic Manual, California Department of Transportation (Caltrans). Thus traffic impacts resulting from
    unusual or unsafe street, intersection or driveway configurations or other anomalies are not expected
    to be a factor in traffic performance in the Project area or surrounding roadways and intersections.




                                                                                                        17
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010




CHAPTER 3. FUTURE CONDITIONS
This chapter details the assessment of future traffic conditions performed assuming the full buildout of the
Coastal Land Use Plan and Coastal Zoning amendments proposed under the Project.

3.1 PROJECT TRIP GENERATION
To analyze the potential for the Project to impact the surrounding street system, and consistent with
                                                                                       th
Article XXVII, trip generation estimates were calculated using Trip Generation, 7 Edition (Institute of
Transportation Engineers [ITE], 2003), the current version of the Trip Generation manual in August, 2007.
Table 9, summarizes the trip generation rates used in this study, and the resulting trip generation
estimates. The following details the justification for the selection of the individual ITE rates used in this
study:

    ·   Shopping Center (ITE Land Use #820): This rate was determined to be the most appropriate rate
        to analyze Coastal Commercial, (CC-1 through CC-5) and Commercial (C-3A and C-5A) land use
        zoning classifications proposed in the Project area. The ITE surveys for the Shopping Center
        rate include surveys of shopping centers with both retail and non-retail uses, such as restaurants,
        banks, health clubs, and recreational facilities. It is anticipated that the foregoing zoning districts
        will have a variety of retail and non-retail uses similar to the mixed-use shopping centers included
        in the ITE surveys. Therefore, the Shopping Center rate is the most appropriate Trip Generation,
          th
        7 Edition rate to apply to these project land use types. The C-5A zoning classification also
        permits light industrial uses and storage uses. Expected development of these uses in C-5A
        zones is separately accounted for in trip generation forecasts for General Light Industrial and
        Mini-Warehouse classes of development discussed below.

    ·   Government Office rate (ITE Land Use #730): This rate was applied to the Government Office
        land use type because it was determined to be the most applicable to this land use type. These
        uses would occur within the Harbor/Pier area, located within CC zones, but are separately
        analyzed due to the different nature of trip generation for these uses.

    ·   General Light Industrial rate (ITE Land Use #110): This rate was applied to the Industrial (I-2A)
        land use type because it was determined to be the most applicable rate to this land use type.
        This rate was applied to industrial use that is permitted in C-5A zones.

    ·   Mini-Warehouse rate (ITE Land Use #151): This rate was applied to the Mini-Warehouse land
        use type because it was determined to be the most applicable rate to this land use type. This use
        is allowed in the C-5A zone.

    ·   County Park rate (ITE Land Use #412): This rate was applied to the park land use type. It is
        anticipated that a potential park at the current AES Power Plant site would be a city park, but the
        City Park rate (ITE Land Use #411) only provides a daily trip rate, and therefore could not be
        used for AM and PM peak hour analysis. Additionally, it is anticipated that the potential park
        would have a mixture of passive open space, and active park space. This is consistent with the
        County Park uses that were served to develop the Land Use #412 daily, AM and PM trips rates.
        Therefore, it was determined that the County Park rate was most applicable rate to this land use
        type.

    ·   Apartment rate (ITE Land Use #220): This rate was applied to the multi-family dwelling unit land
        use type (allowed in the MU-2 mixed use zone) because it was determined to be the most
        applicable and conservative rate for this land use type (condominiums have lower trip generation
        rates). It should be noted that 49 senior residential living units currently exist on the site assigned
        the MU-2 zoning. The amended zoning would allow a maximum of 54 multi-family-dwelling units



                                                                                                            18
   Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
   September 2010



             on the site. For purposes of the traffic analysis, it was assumed that redevelopment with up to 54
             units could occur, resulting in a net increase of 5 units. However, because the senior residential
             units on the property generate substantially less traffic than conventional multi-family dwelling
             units, it was assumed that redevelopment, if it occurs, would result in a substantially greater
             increase in residential traffic than would be normally attributable to 5 new units only. This
             increase was conservatively estimated at 85% of the normal traffic generation for all newly
             developed apartment units on the site, or the equivalent of 46 new apartment units. This figure
             was used to conservatively calculate the net increase in residential traffic that would occur from
             redevelopment with new apartments.

   As indicated in Table 9, the Project is estimated to generate 980 AM and 2,518 PM peak hour trips.

   3.2 PROJECT TRIP DISTRIBUTION
   The geographic distribution of the traffic generated by the Project depends on several factors, including
   the geographic population distribution from which visitors would be drawn and the location of the Project
   in relation to the surrounding street system. To develop a distribution pattern, the Redondo Beach Traffic
   Model (RBTM) developed by Austin-Foust Associates (AFA) was used. Select zone runs of the RBTM



                                                                                                                                 19
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



were conducted for the traffic analysis zones (TAZs) that contain the Project. The select zone runs, track
all trips with an origin or destination in the Project TAZs. The geographic distribution of these trips were
evaluated and generalized to produce the trip distribution pattern illustrated in Figure 4. Details about the
model development are discussed below under Cumulative Base Conditions.

3.3 PROJECT TRAFFIC ASSIGNMENT
The Project trips estimated in the trip generation stage were assigned to the study area street system
using the distribution pattern illustrated in Figure 4. The resulting Project-only AM and PM peak hour
traffic volumes at each of the analyzed intersections are illustrated in Figure 5.

3.4.3   Intersection Impact Analysis

Significant Impact Criteria

To evaluate the potential for the Project to impact the surrounding street system, impact criteria from two
sources were selected:

    ·   First, the planning department of the City of Redondo Beach has tentatively established a
        standard incremental significance threshold to determine if a project creates a significant traffic
        impact. These criteria will be formally adopted with City Council approval of the 2009 Circulation
        Element Update. A project impact on an intersection is deemed significant if any of the following
        occur:

        -        Four percentage point increase in the volume to capacity ratio at an intersection where the
                 baseline intersection condition is LOS C; or




                                                                                                                                25
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



        -   Two percentage point increase in the volume to capacity ratio at an intersection where the
            baseline intersection condition is LOS D; or

        -   One percentage point increase in the volume to capacity ratio at an intersection where the
            baseline intersection condition is LOS E or worse.

    ·   Second, the following additional impact criteria from Article XXVII Section 27.2(c)(1), were applied
        in the impact analysis of the Project. Article XXVII does not explicitly define the criteria for
        determining the "project impacts" under Section 27.4(b), therefore it is reasonable to use the
        "significant increase" criteria provided in Section 27.2(c)(1)):

        -   An increase in intersection capacity utilization (ICU) of 0.01 or more at any critical intersection
            operating at a level of service (LOS) "E" or worse, or having an ICU of 0.9 or higher

        -   Any increase in ICU at any City intersection from less than 0.9 to 0.9 or higher

        -   Any change in LOS at any critical intersection or on any critical corridor from better than "E"
            to "E" or worse.

3.4.5   Qualitative Assessment of As-Built plus Project Conditions

The following details the expected changes to the qualitative operating conditions with the addition of
Project traffic:

    ·   Heavy Vehicle Traffic ­ The Project would modestly increase heavy vehicle traffic, as needed to
        support the typical service and delivery functions of the Project land uses. This increase in heavy
        vehicle traffic would not be expected to impact operating conditions.

    ·   Bus Stops ­ With the addition of Project traffic, queues caused by temporary bus blockages
        would be longer.



                                                                                                        28
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



      ·    Intersection and Corridor Oversaturation (Downstream Traffic Queuing Impacts) ­ With the
           addition of Project traffic, the heavy traffic flows and intersection and corridor oversaturation
           would increase.

      ·    Side Street and Driveway Entrances and Exits ­ With the addition of Project traffic, the length of
           any queues caused by vehicles waiting to turn onto a side street or driveway would increase.
           However, this is not expected to be a regular issue due to the presence of left-turn lanes for turns
           onto most side streets.

      ·    Ingress Staking and Overflowing ­ Issues with ingress staking and overflowing are not anticipated
           with the addition of Project traffic.

      ·    Left-Turn Lane Queuing and Overflow ­ More frequent and longer queue spillback out of left-turn
           lanes would be expected with the addition of Project traffic.

      ·    Traffic, Pedestrian & Bicycle Safety ­ The Project would result in increased traffic traveling on
           streets and through intersections in the study area. However, this increase in traffic is not
           expected to degrade safety, as traffic, pedestrian, and bicycle facilities have been designed to
           relevant safety standards, and any new facilities would continue to be designed to relevant safety
           standards.

In addition to these qualitative observations, some of the traffic concerns addressed above are a factor in
the HCM analysis using the Synchro software package. Delay calculations are affected by traffic volumes
on intersecting side streets, as well as turn lane queuing and overflow at intersections. Impacts on traffic
flow from these factors are therefore reflected in the LOS analysis produced using the HCM methodology.

3.5       CUMULATIVE BASE TRAFFIC CONDITIONS

3.5.1      Model Structure Overview

In order to evaluate the impacts of the Project on the local street system, estimates of future traffic
conditions without the project were developed for the build-out year using the RBTM developed by AFA.
The RBTM is based on the Regional Transportation Model (RTM), which is prepared by the Southern
California Association of Governments (SCAG), and uses a land use database for the City, which
contained all cumulative projects from the last eight years, as well as future projects that are reasonably
foreseeable. AFA also modified SCAG's model within the City to reflect the local network of streets and
create a finer zone system to allow for more detailed forecasts for the City's streets. The RBTM can be
used as a tool for forecasting future average daily traffic and peak hour traffic conditions in the City of
Redondo Beach.

The RBTM can be divided into four components:

      ·    Highway Network Definition

      ·    Trip Generation

      ·    RTM interface (trip distribution)

      ·    Traffic Assignment

These components follow that of typical travel demand models. The highway network definition assigns
attributes, such as speed and distance, to the local street network that will accommodate the traffic to be
forecasted. Trip generation utilizes a land use database for land use inputs within the City. Trip
generation is calculated for the area as daily vehicle trips, and AM and PM peak hour trips. Existing and
future SCAG RTM vehicle trip patterns are used to derive the trip distribution component. The traffic


                                                                                                            29
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



assignment component of the RBTM uses a process that considers network capacity, and includes a
post-processing step that helps enhance the accuracy of the forecasts. The RBTM provides forecasts for
intersections in Redondo Beach. AFA also conducted year (2009) model validation (City of Redondo
Beach Traffic Model: Model Description and Validation [Austin-Foust Associates, 2008]).

3.5.2   Intersection Traffic Volumes

To forecast Cumulative Base traffic conditions, a full run of the RBTM was performed using 2030 land use
data from the Circulation Element Update. A select zone run was then performed for the Project TAZs,
enabling all trips to or from the Project TAZs to be tracked. Trips from future land use in the Project TAZs
were unassigned from the forecast volumes at each study intersection. By unassigning these volumes,
the Cumulative Base scenario reflects As-Built Conditions for the Project TAZs, but the future land use at
all other TAZs. This way, the Project can be tested with an accurate cumulative baseline. For a few
select movements, certain Cumulative Base volumes would have fallen below existing volumes using this
unassignment procedure. However, in this analysis, volumes were not allowed to drop below As-Built
Conditions levels. AM and PM peak hour Cumulative Base traffic volumes are illustrated in Figure 7.

3.5.3    Intersection Levels of Service

to determine the Cumulative Base operating conditions at the eight study intersections. LOS calculation
worksheets are included in Appendix C.

3.5.5   Qualitative Assessment of Cumulative Base Conditions

The following details the changes to the qualitative operating conditions with the addition of traffic from
background and cumulative development:

    ·   Heavy Vehicle Traffic ­ With the addition of cumulative land use, heavy vehicle traffic would be
        expected to modestly increase to support the day-to-day deliver and haul needs for the
        cumulative land use. This would not be expected to negatively impact operating conditions.




                                                                                                        34
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



    ·   Bus Stops ­ With the addition of cumulative traffic, queues caused by temporary bus blockages
        would be longer.

    ·   Intersection and Corridor Oversaturation (Downstream Traffic Queuing Impacts) ­ With the
        addition of cumulative traffic, heavy traffic flows and intersection and corridor oversaturation
        would increase.

    ·   Side Street and Driveway Entrances and Exits ­ With the addition of cumulative traffic, the length
        of any queues caused by vehicles waiting to turn onto a side street or driveway would increase.
        However, unless particular cumulative developments attract significant traffic into or out of their
        driveways, this is not expected to be a regular issue due to the presence of left-turn lanes for
        turns onto most side streets.

    ·   Ingress Staking and Overflowing ­ Issues with ingress staking and overflowing are not anticipated
        with the addition of cumulative traffic, unless particular developments attract heavy inbound or
        outbound traffic at their driveways that could overflow.

    ·   Left-Turn Lane Queuing and Overflow ­ More frequent and longer queue spillback out of left-turn
        lanes would be expected with the addition of cumulative traffic.

    ·   Traffic, Pedestrian & Bicycle Safety ­ The Project would result in increased traffic traveling on
        streets and through intersections in the study area. However, this increase in traffic is not
        expected to degrade safety, as traffic, pedestrian, and bicycle facilities have been designed to
        relevant safety standards, and any new facilities would continue to be designed to relevant safety
        standards.

3.7       FUTURE CONDITIONS WITH MITIGATION

3.7.1     Mitigation Program

Attempts were made to devise mitigation programs for all five impacted intersections. The mitigation
programs aimed at identifying physical measures that would mitigate intersection impacts to less than
significant levels. However, only one feasible physical mitigation measure was identified:

      7) PCH & Torrance Boulevard ­ A physical improvement is proposed (widening and restriping of
         northbound approach to include one left-turn lane, two through lanes, and one right-turn lane).
         This measure was proposed mitigation in the Circulation Element.

No feasible physical mitigation measures were indentified at any other study intersections.

While operational mitigation measures, including signal timing optimization, could benefit operations at
impacted intersections, because the ICU methodology is a volume to capacity analysis, the operational
benefits of signal optimization cannot be quantified with ICU, and have therefore not been analyzed.




                                                                                                            40
 Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
 September 2010



 3.7.2       As-Built plus Project Conditions with Mitigation Levels of Service and Impact Analysis

 Table 20 details As-Built plus Project Conditions with Mitigations for the ICU methodology.

 Because the ICU methodology is a volume to capacity analysis, the operational benefits of signal
 optimization cannot be quantified with ICU. Therefore, the only measure that can be quantified using ICU
 is the physical mitigation measure proposed at Intersection 7. As Table 20 indicates, the mitigation
 measure would reduce the overall ICU at this intersection to an LOS D or better during the AM peak hour,
 thus mitigating that peak hour, but would not reduce the PM peak hour ICU to an LOS D or better during
 the PM hour, so would remain impacted during that peak hour according to the impact criteria set forth in
 the Circulation Element and Article XXVII. All other intersections would remain impacted under As-Built
 plus Project Conditions using the ICU methodology.

CHAPTER 4. SUMMARY
This study was undertaken to analyze the potential traffic impacts of the proposed Coastal Land Use Plan
and Coastal Zoning Amendments for the Harbor/Pier, AES Power Plant and Catalina Avenue corridor
aresa of the City of Redondo Beach. The following summarizes the results of this analysis:

    ·   The Project would allow the potential for up to 940,215 net new sf of mixed-use commercial land
        uses of varying intensities, government office, industrial, and mini-warehouse. It would also allow
        for a minor increase in residential development on one parcel, and for additional park space. For
        purposes of the traffic analysis, it was assumed that the increase in active use park space would
        be up to 24.7 acres. The total number of trips generated by the Project if fully built out is 30,270
        daily trips, 980 AM peak hour, and 2,518 PM peak hour trips.

    ·   Eight intersections were selected for weekday AM and PM peak hour analysis using both the ICU
        and HCM methodologies, as dictated in the City of Redondo Beach Circulation Element and
        Article XXVII.

    ·   Nine roadways segments within 3,000 feet of the Project site were also selected for analysis
        using the HCM Urban Arterials methodology.

    ·   Under As-Built conditions, four of eight study intersections operate at LOS E or worse during one
        or both peak hours using the ICU methodology. The remaining intersections operate at an
        acceptable LOS D or better. Using the HCM methodologies, one intersection operates at LOS F
        during the PM peak hour, and all other intersections operate at an acceptable LOS during both
        peak hours. Using the HCM Urban Arterials methodology, all analyzed roadway segments
        operate at LOS E or worse.

    ·   Under As-Built plus Project Conditions, five of eight study intersections are projected to operate at
        LOS E or worse during one or both peak hours using the ICU methodology. The remaining
        intersections are projected to operate at an acceptable LOS D or better. Using the HCM
        methodologies, two study intersections are projected to operate at LOS E or worse during the PM
        peak hour. All other intersections operate at an acceptable LOS during both peak hours. Using
        the HCM Urban Arterials methodology, all analyzed roadway segments are projected to operate
        at LOS E or worse. The Project is expected to impact five intersections according to impact
        criteria from the City of Redondo Beach Circulation Element and Article XXVII.

    ·   Under Cumulative Base conditions, four of eight study intersections are projected to operate at
        LOS E or worse during one or both peak hours using the ICU methodology. The remaining
        intersections are projected to operate at an acceptable LOS D or better. Using the HCM
        methodologies, three intersections are projected to operate at LOS E or worse during one or both
        peak hours, and all other intersections are projected to operate at an acceptable LOS during both
        peak hours. Using the HCM Urban Arterials methodology, all analyzed roadway segments are
        projected to operate at LOS E or worse.

    ·   Under Cumulative plus Project conditions, five of eight study intersections are projected to operate
        at LOS E or worse during one or both peak hours using the ICU methodology. The remaining
        intersections are projected to operate at an acceptable LOS D or better. Using the HCM
        methodologies, four study intersections are projected to operate at LOS E or worse during one or
        both peak hours. All other intersections operate at an acceptable LOS during both peak hours.
        Using the HCM Urban Arterials methodology, all analyzed roadway segments are projected to
        operate at LOS E or worse. The Project is expected to impact five intersections based on impact
        criteria from the City of Redondo Beach Circulation Element and Measure DD.




                                                                                                          43
Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010



    ·   One physical mitigation measure was developed that would partially mitigate the Project impact on
        one study intersection. No feasible physical mitigation measures were indentified at any other study
        intersections.

    ·   Field observations indicated existing queuing and corridor oversaturation in the study area that
        would be worsened with the addition of traffic from the Project.

Traffic Study for the Harbor and Pier Zoning Amendments, City of Redondo Beach
September 2010

     APPENDIX D:
"WORST CASE" ANALYSIS
Appendix D: "Worst Case" Analysis
August 2010




1.1     "WORST CASE" DEVELOPMENT ANALYSIS

1.1.1   Analysis Approach

A sensitivity test was conducted to determine the maximum development potential of the Harbor/Pier
parcels before this theoretical maximum development would degrade intersection operation by an LOS
grade.

To analyze this potential, this sensitivity test was conducted for the As-Built plus Project scenario as
detailed in the body of this report. The intersection of Catalina Avenue and Beryl Street was selected for
this sensitivity test because it is the main gateway intersection to the Harbor Pier parcels. As presented
in Table 10 of the body of this report, the intersection is projected to operate at LOS E under As-Built plus
Project Conditions.

The intersection was evaluated to determine the critical moves that would most affect the LOS at the
intersection. The key critical moves during the PM peak hour are the eastbound right move (outbound
traffic from the Harbor/Pier parcels), and the northbound left move (inbound traffic to the Harbor/Pier
parcels). These critical moves were tested to determine the overall increase in Project trips that could
occur before the intersection would degrade to LOS F under As-Built plus Project Conditions.

1.1.2   Results

The project trip generation could increase by 200 trips total (approximately 14%) before the intersection
would degrade to LOS F under As-Built plus Project Conditions, representing approximately 53,300
square feet of additional commercial development in the Harbor/Pier parcels (for a total of 418,300
square feet of commercial development, and 35,000 square feet of government office).




                                                                                                           1
                  APPENDIX E:
BUILDOUT UNDER ALL EXISTING REGULATIONS ANALYSIS
Appendix E: All Existing Regulations Analysis
September 2010




CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
This analysis assesses the potential traffic impacts of buildout in the project area (LCP Area 2) taking into
account all existing adopted and legally effective land use regulations. These regulations include the
existing Coastal Land Use Plan, the current zoning analyzed in the body of the report, and additional City
policies and development standards that apply to the area under the City's current general plan and the
Harbor Civic Center Specific Plan. This analysis differs from the comparative analysis for the existing
Coastal Land Use Plan and zoning conducted in the previous section in that this analysis takes into
account additional policies and restrictions found in the general plan and specific plan which would apply
to development in the project area, whether or not the proposed Coastal Land Use Plan and Coastal
Zoning Ordinance amendments are approved by the voters. For reference, this analysis is referred to as
the "All Existing Regulations" analysis.

1.1 APPLICABLE REGULATIONS
The All Existing Regulations analysis assumes that the proposed Coastal Land Use Plan and Coastal
Zoning Ordinance amendments are not passed, and all existing, legally effective City planning policies,
standards and zoning regulations remain in effect. These policies, standards and regulations include
applicable provisions of the City's general plan, the Harbor Civic Center Specific Plan, the Coastal Land
Use Plan and zoning. Where conflicts between the relevant planning documents exist, it has been
assumed that the most restrictive regulations would apply, consistent with past City practice. For
example, although the zoning assumed to be in effect based on a recent court decision permits virtually
any land use in the Harbor-Pier area that is allowed anywhere in the City under the zoning code, it is
assumed that allowable land uses are limited to those authorized by the Coastal Land Use Plan and
adopted Specific Plan. Table 1 details the general types of land uses permitted by existing regulations,
the land areas authorized for these uses, and the traffic generation rates assigned to these uses for
purposes of the traffic analysis.

1.2 STUDY SCOPE
This All Existing Regulations comparison utilizes the same existing baseline conditions (As-Built
Conditions, and Cumulative Base conditions) as the Project analysis. Thus, the traffic impact analysis
was conducted for the following:

    ·   As-Built plus Buildout under All Existing Regulations­ This analysis assesses changes in traffic
        conditions resulting from traffic reasonably expected to be generated by buildout of the project
        area under existing regulations, and assuming no substantial future changes in allowable
        development. The incremental impacts of this buildout scenario on As-Built traffic operating
        conditions were then identified.

    ·   Cumulative Base plus Buildout under All Existing Regulations­ This analysis assess changes in
        2030 cumulative traffic conditions resulting from traffic reasonably expected to be generated by
        buildout of the project area under existing regulations. The incremental impacts of the alternative
        on future traffic operating conditions were then identified.

The same study area and intersections and roadway segments as the Project were analyzed for the All
Existing Regulations Analysis.

1.3 ORGANIZATION OF APPENDIX REPORT
This appendix is divided into four chapters, including this introduction. Chapter 2 provides the As-Built
plus Buildout Under All Existing Regulations assessment. Chapter 3 provides the Cumulative Base plus
Buildout Under All Existing Regulations assessment. Chapter 4 compares the results of the Buildout
Under All Existing Regulations to the Project analysis.

the As-Built peak hour traffic volumes to develop As-Built plus Buildout Under All Existing Regulations
traffic volumes. This scenario was developed to analyze the incremental impacts of the Buildout Under
All Existing Regulations on As-Built traffic operating conditions.

Chapter 2 of the main report to determine the As-Built plus Buildout Under All Existing Regulations
operating conditions at the eight study intersections.

methodology. Based on the ICU analysis, the following intersections are projected to operate at LOS E or
worse during one or both peak hours:

    1) PCH & Herondo Street/Anita Street (both peak hours)
    2) PCH & Catalina Avenue (PM peak hour)
    4) PCH & Beryl Street (both peak hours)
    5) Catalina Avenue & Beryl Street (PM peak hour)
    7) PCH & Torrance Boulevard (both peak hours)

All other intersections are projected to operate at an acceptable LOS D or better during both peak hours.

Table 4 summarizes the results of the AM and PM peak hour intersection analysis using the HCM
methodologies. Based on the HCM analyses, the following intersections are projected to operate at LOS
E or F during the PM peak hour:

    1) PCH & Herondo Street/Anita Street
    3) Catalina Avenue & Francisca Avenue
    5) Catalina Avenue & Beryl Street

All other intersections are projected to operate at an acceptable LOS D or better during both peak hours.

Daily alternative segment volumes were forecast using the same approach described within the main

segments are forecast to operate at LOS E or worse under As-Built plus Buildout Under All Existing
Regulations.

2.3.5   Qualitative Assessment of As-Built plus Buildout Under All Existing Regulations
        Conditions

The following details the changes to the qualitative operating conditions with the addition of the Buildout
Under All Existing Regulations traffic:

    ·   Heavy Vehicle Traffic ­ Heavy vehicle traffic would be expected to marginally increase to support
        the day-to-day delivery and haul needs of the land use that could potentially be built with Buildout
        Under All Existing Regulations. This marginal increase in heavy vehicle traffic would not be
        expected to negatively impact traffic operations.

    ·   Bus Stops ­ With the addition of traffic with Buildout Under All Existing Regulations, queues
        caused by temporary bus blockages would be longer.

    ·   Intersection and Corridor Oversaturation (Downstream Traffic Queuing Impacts) ­ With the
        addition of traffic with Buildout Under All Existing Regulations, intersection and corridor
        oversaturation would increase.

    ·   Side Street and Driveway Entrances and Exits ­ With the addition of traffic with Buildout Under All
        Existing Regulations, the length of any queues caused by vehicles waiting to turn onto a side
        street or driveway would increase. However, this is not expected to be a regular issue due to the
        presence of left-turn lanes for turns onto most side streets.

    ·   Ingress Staking and Overflowing ­ Issues with ingress staking and overflowing are not anticipated
        with the addition of traffic with Buildout Under All Existing Regulations.

    ·   Left-Turn Lane Queuing and Overflow ­ More frequent and longer queue spillback out of left-turn
        lanes would be expected with the addition of traffic with Buildout Under All Existing Regulations .

    ·   Traffic, Pedestrian & Bicycle Safety ­ Buildout Under All Existing Regulations would result in
        increased traffic traveling on streets and through intersections in the study area. However, this
        increase in traffic is not expected to degrade safety, as traffic, pedestrian, and bicycle facilities
        have been designed to relevant safety standards, and any new facilities would continue to be
        designed to relevant safety standards.

              vehicles at the intersection.


3.1.3   Impact Analysis

Impact Analysis

The impact criteria detailed in the main report were used to evaluate the LOS and incremental V/C
increase of Buildout Under All Existing Regulations. As indicated in Table 6, Cumulative Base plus
Buildout Under All Existing Regulations is expected to impact the following study intersections during one
or both peak hours:

    1) PCH & Herondo Street/Anita Street (both peak hours)
    2) PCH & Catalina Avenue (PM peak hour)
    4) PCH & Beryl Street (both peak hours)
    5) Catalina Avenue & Beryl Street (PM peak hour)



                                                                                                                               11
Appendix E: All Existing Regulations Analysis
September 2010



    7) PCH & Torrance Boulevard (both peak hours)

3.1.4   Segment Volumes and Level of Service

Buildout Under All Existing Regulations segment volumes were added to the Cumulative Base daily
segment volumes described to develop Cumulative Base plus Buildout Under All Existing Regulations
daily segment volumes. These volumes were analyzed using the HCM Urban Arterials methodology
consistent with the analysis of the Project in the main report. As seen in Table 8, the LOS is expected to
remain the same for each segment with the addition of the Buildout Under All Existing Regulations' traffic,
with the exception of Prospect Avenue, which would degrade to LOS F. All segments are projected to
operate at LOS E or worse.

3.1.5   Qualitative Assessment of Cumulative plus Buildout Under All Existing Regulations
        Conditions

The following details the changes to the qualitative operating conditions with the addition of Buildout
Under All Existing Regulations traffic:

    ·   Heavy Vehicle Traffic ­ Heavy vehicle traffic would be expected to marginally increase to support
        the day-to-day delivery and haul needs of the land use that could potentially be built with Buildout
        Under All Existing Regulations. This marginal increase in heavy vehicle traffic would not be
        expected to negatively impact traffic operations.

    ·   Bus Stops ­ With the addition of the Buildout Under All Existing Regulations traffic, queues
        caused by temporary bus blockages would be longer in length.

    ·   Intersection and Corridor Oversaturation (Downstream Traffic Queuing Impacts) ­ With the
        addition of the Buildout Under All Existing Regulations traffic, intersection and corridor
        oversaturation would increase.

    ·   Side Street and Driveway Entrances and Exits ­ With the addition of the Buildout Under All
        Existing Regulations traffic, the length of any queues caused by vehicles waiting to turn onto a
        side street or driveway would increase. However, this is not expected to be a regular issue due
        to the presence of left-turn lanes for turns onto most side streets.

    ·   Ingress Staking and Overflowing ­ Issues with ingress staking and overflowing are not anticipated
        with the addition of the Buildout Under All Existing Regulations traffic.

    ·   Left-Turn Lane Queuing and Overflow ­ More frequent and longer queue spillback out of left-turn
        lanes would be expected with the addition of the Buildout Under All Existing Regulations traffic.

    ·   Traffic, Pedestrian & Bicycle Safety ­ The Buildout Under All Existing Regulations traffic would
        result in increased traffic traveling on streets and through intersections in the study area.
        However, this increase in traffic is not expected to degrade safety, as traffic, pedestrian, and
        bicycle facilities have been designed to relevant safety standards, and any new facilities would
        continue to be designed to relevant safety standards.

3.2 FUTURE CONDITIONS WITH MITIGATION

3.2.1   Mitigation Program

Attempts were made to devise mitigation programs for all five impacted intersections. The mitigation
programs aimed at identifying physical measures that would mitigate intersection impacts to less than
significant levels. However, only one feasible physical mitigation measure was identified:




                                                                                                  13
 Appendix E: All Existing Regulations Analysis
 September 2010



      7) PCH & Torrance Boulevard ­ A physical improvement is proposed (widening of northbound
         approach to include one left-turn lane, two through lanes, and one right-turn lane). This measure
         was proposed mitigation in the Circulation Element.

 No feasible physical mitigation measures were indentified at any other study intersections.

 While operational mitigation measures, including signal timing optimization, could benefit operations at
 impacted intersections, because the ICU methodology is a volume to capacity analysis, the operational
 benefits of signal optimization cannot be quantified with ICU, and have therefore not been analyzed.

 3.2.2     As-Built plus Buildout Under All Existing Regulations with Mitigation Levels of Service and
           Impact Analysis

 Table 9 details As-Built plus Buildout Under All Existing Regulations Conditions with Mitigations. As the
 table indicates, the mitigation measure would reduce the overall ICU at this intersection to an LOS D or
 better during the AM peak hour, thus mitigating that peak hour, but would not reduce the PM peak hour
 ICU to an LOS D or better during the PM hour, so would remain impacted during that peak hour
 according to the impact criteria set forth in the Circulation Element and Article XXVII. All other
 intersections would remain impacted under As-Built plus Buildout Under All Existing Regulations
 Conditions using the ICU methodology.

4.3 INTERSECTION IMPACTS COMPARISON
As indicated in Table 16, there are no additional intersection impacts with Buildout Under All Existing
Regulations, than with the Project as analyzed in the main report. While Buildout Under All Existing
Regulations would add more traffic to intersections than the Project, because these intersections are
already projected to be impacted with the Project, this further degradation does not lead to additional
impacts.
